<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Pelagic fish</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moofushi_Kandu_fish.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1224" data-file-width="1632" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Moofushi_Kandu_fish.jpg/300px-Moofushi_Kandu_fish.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Moofushi_Kandu_fish.jpg/450px-Moofushi_Kandu_fish.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Moofushi_Kandu_fish.jpg/600px-Moofushi_Kandu_fish.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moofushi_Kandu_fish.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling" title="Shoaling and schooling">school</a> of large pelagic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator_fish" title="Predator fish">predator fish</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bluefin_trevally" title="Bluefin trevally">bluefin trevally</a>) sizing up a school of small pelagic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prey_fish" title="Prey fish">prey fish</a> (<a href="/wiki/Anchovy" title="Anchovy">anchovies</a>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Pelagic fish</b> live in the <a href="/wiki/Pelagic_zone" title="Pelagic zone">pelagic zone</a> of ocean or lake waters – being neither close to the bottom nor near the shore – in contrast with <a href="/wiki/Demersal_fish" title="Demersal fish">demersal fish</a>, which do live on or near the bottom, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reef_fish" title="Reef fish">reef fish</a>, which are associated with <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">coral reefs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lal8_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lal8-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The marine <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelagic" title="Pelagic">pelagic</a> environment is the largest aquatic habitat on Earth, occupying 1,370 million cubic kilometres (330 million cubic miles), and is the habitat for 11% of known fish species. The <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a> have a mean depth of 4000 metres. About 98% of the total water volume is below 100 metres (330 ft), and 75% is below 1,000 metres (3,300 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle585_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle585-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Marine pelagic fish can be divided into pelagic coastal fish and oceanic pelagic fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coastal_fish#Coastal_pelagic_fish" title="Coastal fish">Coastal fish</a> inhabit the relatively shallow and sunlit waters above the <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a>, while oceanic fish (which may well also swim inshore) inhabit the vast and deep waters beyond the continental shelf.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pelagic fish range in size from small coastal <a href="/wiki/Forage_fish" title="Forage fish">forage fish</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">herrings</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sardine" title="Sardine">sardines</a>, to large <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">apex predator</a> oceanic fishes, such as <a href="/wiki/Bluefin_tuna" title="Bluefin tuna">bluefin tuna</a> and oceanic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sharks" title="Sharks">sharks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lal8_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lal8-1">[1]</a></sup> They are usually agile swimmers with streamlined bodies, capable of sustained cruising on long-distance <a href="/wiki/Fish_migration" title="Fish migration">migrations</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Indo-Pacific_sailfish" title="Indo-Pacific sailfish">Indo-Pacific sailfish</a>, an oceanic pelagic fish, can sprint at over 110 kilometres per hour. Some tuna species cruise across the Pacific Ocean. Many pelagic fish swim in <a href="/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling" title="Shoaling and schooling">schools</a> weighing hundreds of tonnes. Others are solitary, like the large <a href="/wiki/Ocean_sunfish" title="Ocean sunfish">ocean sunfish</a> weighing over 500 kilograms, which sometimes drift passively with ocean currents, eating <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lal8_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-Lal8-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epipelagic_fish">Epipelagic fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelagic_fish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Epipelagic fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bluefin-big.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1140" data-file-width="1728" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Bluefin-big.jpg/220px-Bluefin-big.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Bluefin-big.jpg/330px-Bluefin-big.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Bluefin-big.jpg/440px-Bluefin-big.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Large epipelagic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator_fish" title="Predator fish">predator fish</a>, like this <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_bluefin_tuna" title="Atlantic bluefin tuna">Atlantic bluefin tuna</a>, have a deeply forked tail and a smooth body shaped like a spindle tapered at both ends and <a href="/wiki/Countershading" title="Countershading">countershaded</a> with silvery colours.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herring2.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="81" data-file-width="211" height="84" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/25/Herring2.jpg" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Small epipelagic <a href="/wiki/Forage_fish" title="Forage fish">forage fish</a>, like this <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_herring" title="Atlantic herring">Atlantic herring</a>, share the same body features listed for the predator fish above</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Epipelagic fish inhabit the epipelagic zone. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epipelagic_zone" title="Epipelagic zone">epipelagic zone</a> is the water from the surface of the sea down to 200 metres. It is also referred to as the <b>surface waters</b> or the <b>sunlit zone</b>, and includes the <a href="/wiki/Photic_zone" title="Photic zone">photic zone</a>. The photic zone is defined as the surface waters down to the point where the sunlight has attenuated to 1% of the surface value. This depth depends on how <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turbid" title="Turbid">turbid</a> the water is, but in clear water can extend to 200 metres, coinciding with the epipelagic zone. The <a href="/wiki/Photic_zone" title="Photic zone">photic zone</a> has sufficient light for <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photosynthese" title="Photosynthese">photosynthese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle571_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle571-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>The epipelagic zone is vast, and is the home for most pelagic fish. The zone is well lit so visual predators can use their eyesight, is usually well mixed and oxygenated from wave action, and can be a good habitat for algae to grow. However, it is an almost featureless habitat. This lack of habitat diversity results in a lack of <a href="/wiki/Species_diversity" title="Species diversity">species diversity</a>, so the zone supports less than 2% of the world's known fish species. Much of the zone lacks nutrients for supporting fish, so epipelagic fish tend to be found in coastal water above the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_shelves" title="Continental shelves">continental shelves</a>, where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_runoff" title="Land runoff">land runoff</a> can provide nutrients, or in those parts of the ocean where <a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">upwelling</a> moves nutrients into the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle571_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle571-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Epipelagic fish can be broadly divided into small <a href="/wiki/Forage_fish" title="Forage fish">forage fish</a> and larger <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator_fish" title="Predator fish">predator fish</a> which feed on them. Forage fish <a href="/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling" title="Shoaling and schooling">school</a> and <a href="/wiki/Filter_feeder" title="Filter feeder">filter feed</a> on <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a>. Most epipelagic fish have streamlined bodies capable of sustained cruising on <a href="/wiki/Fish_migration" title="Fish migration">migrations</a>. In general, predatory and forage fish share the same <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphological</a> features. Predator fish are usually <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fusiform" title="wikt:fusiform">fusiform</a> with large mouths, smooth bodies, and deeply forked tails. Many use vision to predate zooplankton or smaller fish, while others <a href="/wiki/Filter_feeder" title="Filter feeder">filter feed</a> on <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herring_Silvering.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="568" data-file-width="306" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Herring_Silvering.svg/160px-Herring_Silvering.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Herring_Silvering.svg/240px-Herring_Silvering.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Herring_Silvering.svg/320px-Herring_Silvering.svg.png 2x" width="160"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herring_Silvering.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">Herring</a> reflectors are nearly vertical for camouflage from the side.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most epipelagic predator fish and their smaller prey fish are <a href="/wiki/Countershading" title="Countershading">countershaded</a> with silvery colours which reduce visibility by scattering incoming light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle571_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle571-5">[5]</a></sup> The silvering is achieved with reflective <a href="/wiki/Fish_scale" title="Fish scale">fish scales</a> that function as small mirrors. This can give an effect of transparency. At medium depths at sea, light comes from above, so a mirror oriented vertically makes animals such as fish invisible from the side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herring2002_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herring2002-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the shallower epipelagic waters, the mirrors must reflect a mixture of wavelengths, and the fish accordingly has crystal stacks with a range of different spacings. A further complication for fish with bodies that are rounded in cross-section is that the mirrors would be ineffective if laid flat on the skin, as they would fail to reflect horizontally. The overall mirror effect is achieved with many small reflectors, all oriented vertically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herring2002_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Herring2002-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Though the number of species is limited, epipelagic fishes are abundant. What they lack in diversity they make up in numbers. Forage fish occur in huge numbers, and large fish that prey on them are often sought after as premier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fish_(food)" title="Fish (food)">food fish</a>. As a group, epipelagic fishes form the most valuable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fisheries" title="Fisheries">fisheries</a> in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle571_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle571-5">[5]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many forage fish are facultative predators that can pick individual <a href="/wiki/Copepod" title="Copepod">copepods</a> or fish larvae out of the water column, and then change to filter feeding on <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a> when energetically that gives better results. Filter feeding fish usually use long fine <a href="/wiki/Gill_raker" title="Gill raker">gill rakers</a> to strain small organisms from the water column. Some of the largest epipelagic fishes, such as the <a href="/wiki/Basking_shark" title="Basking shark">basking shark</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whale_shark" title="Whale shark">whale shark</a> are filter feeders, and so are some of the smallest, such as adult <a href="/wiki/Sprat" title="Sprat">sprats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anchovy" title="Anchovy">anchovies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle572_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle572-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ocean waters that are exceptionally clear contain little food. Areas of high productivity tend to be somewhat turbid from <a href="/wiki/Algal_bloom" title="Algal bloom">plankton blooms</a>. These attract the filter feeding plankton eaters, which in turn attract the higher predators. Tuna fishing tends to be optimum when water turbidity, measured by the maximum depth a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secchi_disc" title="Secchi disc">secchi disc</a> can be seen during a sunny day, is 15 to 35 metres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Floating_objects">Floating objects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelagic_fish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Floating objects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sargassum_weeds_closeup.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Sargassum_weeds_closeup.jpg/220px-Sargassum_weeds_closeup.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Sargassum_weeds_closeup.jpg/330px-Sargassum_weeds_closeup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Sargassum_weeds_closeup.jpg/440px-Sargassum_weeds_closeup.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Drifting <i><a href="/wiki/Sargassum" title="Sargassum">Sargassum</a></i> seaweed provides food and shelter for small epipelagic fish. The small round spheres are floats filled with carbon dioxide which provide buoyancy to the algae.</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Barracuda_off_the_Netherland_Antilles.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Great_Barracuda_off_the_Netherland_Antilles.jpg/220px-Great_Barracuda_off_the_Netherland_Antilles.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Great_Barracuda_off_the_Netherland_Antilles.jpg/330px-Great_Barracuda_off_the_Netherland_Antilles.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Great_Barracuda_off_the_Netherland_Antilles.jpg/440px-Great_Barracuda_off_the_Netherland_Antilles.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Great_barracuda" title="Great barracuda">Great barracuda</a> accompanied by a school of <a href="/wiki/Carangidae" title="Carangidae">jacks</a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:224px;max-width:224px">
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lines_of_Sargassum.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="520" data-file-width="390" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Lines_of_Sargassum.jpg/220px-Lines_of_Sargassum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Lines_of_Sargassum.jpg/330px-Lines_of_Sargassum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4e/Lines_of_Sargassum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Lines of <i>Sargassum</i> can stretch for miles along the ocean surface</div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="margin:1px;width:222px;max-width:222px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Histrio_histrio_by_A._H._Baldwin.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2383" data-file-width="3255" height="161" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Histrio_histrio_by_A._H._Baldwin.jpg/220px-Histrio_histrio_by_A._H._Baldwin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Histrio_histrio_by_A._H._Baldwin.jpg/330px-Histrio_histrio_by_A._H._Baldwin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Histrio_histrio_by_A._H._Baldwin.jpg/440px-Histrio_histrio_by_A._H._Baldwin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The beautifully camouflaged <a href="/wiki/Sargassum_fish" title="Sargassum fish">sargassum fish</a> has evolved to live among drifting <i>Sargassum</i> seaweed</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Epipelagic fish are fascinated with floating objects. They aggregate in considerable numbers around objects such as drifting flotsam, rafts, jellyfish and floating seaweed. The objects appear to provide a "visual stimulus in an optical void".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Floating objects can offer some protection for <a href="/wiki/Juvenile_fish" title="Juvenile fish">juvenile fish</a> from predators. An abundance of drifting seaweed or jellyfish can result in significant increases in the survival rates of some juvenile species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many coastal juveniles use seaweed for the shelter and the food that is available from invertebrates and other fish associated with it. Drifting seaweed, particularly the pelagic <i><a href="/wiki/Sargassum" title="Sargassum">Sargassum</a></i>, provide a niche habitat with its own shelter and food, and even supports its own unique fauna, such as the <a href="/wiki/Sargassum_fish" title="Sargassum fish">sargassum fish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle572_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle572-7">[7]</a></sup> One study, off Florida, found 54 species from 23 families living in flotsam from <i>Sargassum</i> mats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Jellyfish are also used by juvenile fish for shelter and food, even though jellyfish may prey on small fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle576_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle576-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mobile oceanic species such as <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a> can be captured by travelling long distances in large <a href="/wiki/Fishing_vessel" title="Fishing vessel">fishing vessels</a>. A simpler alternative is to leverage off the fascination fish have with floating objects. When fishermen use such objects, they are called <a href="/wiki/Fish_aggregating_device" title="Fish aggregating device">fish aggregating devices</a> (FADs). FADs are anchored rafts or objects of any type, floating on the surface or just below it. Fishermen in the Pacific and Indian oceans set up floating FADs, assembled from all sorts of debris, around tropical islands, and then use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Purse_seine" title="Purse seine">purse seines</a> to capture the fish attracted to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle574.2F5_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle574.2F5-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>A study using <a href="/wiki/Fisheries_acoustics" title="Fisheries acoustics">sonar</a> in French Polynesia, found large shoals of juvenile <a href="/wiki/Bigeye_tuna" title="Bigeye tuna">bigeye tuna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yellowfin_tuna" title="Yellowfin tuna">yellowfin tuna</a> aggregated closest to the devices, 10 to 50 m. Further out, 50 to 150 m, was a less dense group of larger yellowfin and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albacore_tuna" title="Albacore tuna">albacore tuna</a>. Yet further out, to 500 m, was a dispersed group of various large adult tuna. The distribution and density of these groups was variable and overlapped. The FADs were also used by other fish, and the aggregations dispersed when it was dark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Larger fish, even predator fish such as the <a href="/wiki/Great_barracuda" title="Great barracuda">great barracuda</a> in the photo on the left, often attract a retinue of small fish that accompany them in a strategically safe way. <a href="/wiki/Scuba_diving" title="Scuba diving">Skindivers</a> who remain for long periods in the water, also often attract a retinue of fish, with smaller fishes coming in close, and larger fishes observing from a greater distance. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_turtle" title="Marine turtle">Marine turtles</a>, functioning as a mobile shelter for small fish, can be impaled accidentally by a swordfish trying to catch the fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coastal_fish">Coastal fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelagic_fish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Coastal fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Coastal_fish" title="Coastal fish">Coastal fish</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sixfinger_threadfin_school.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="720" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Sixfinger_threadfin_school.jpg/220px-Sixfinger_threadfin_school.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Sixfinger_threadfin_school.jpg/330px-Sixfinger_threadfin_school.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Sixfinger_threadfin_school.jpg/440px-Sixfinger_threadfin_school.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sixfinger_threadfin_school.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Schooling <a href="/wiki/Threadfin" title="Threadfin">threadfin</a>, a coastal species</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Coastal_fish" title="Coastal fish">Coastal fish</a> (also called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neritic" title="Neritic">neritic</a> or inshore fish) inhabit the waters near the <a href="/wiki/Coast" title="Coast">coast</a> and above the <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a>. Since the continental shelf is usually less than 200 metres deep, it follows that coastal fish that are not demersal fish are usually epipelagic fish, inhabiting the sunlit epipelagic zone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle585_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle585-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Coastal epipelagic fish are among the most abundant in the world. They include forage fish as well as the predator fish that feed on them. Forage fish thrive in those inshore waters where high productivity results from the upwelling and shoreline run off of nutrients. Some are partial residents that spawn in streams, estuaries and bays, but most complete their life cycle in the zone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle572_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle572-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Oceanic_fish">Oceanic fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelagic_fish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Oceanic fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oceanic_divisions.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="835" data-file-width="1114" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Oceanic_divisions.svg/400px-Oceanic_divisions.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Oceanic_divisions.svg/600px-Oceanic_divisions.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Oceanic_divisions.svg/800px-Oceanic_divisions.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oceanic_divisions.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Oceanic fish inhabit the <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_zone" title="Oceanic zone">oceanic zone</a>, which is the deep open water which lies beyond the continental shelves.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Oceanic fish (also called open ocean or offshore fish) live in the waters that are not above the continental shelf. Oceanic fish can be contrasted with <a href="/wiki/Coastal_fish" title="Coastal fish">coastal fish</a>, who do live above the continental shelf. However, the two types are not mutually exclusive, since there are no firm boundaries between coastal and ocean regions, and many epipelagic fish move between coastal and oceanic waters, particularly in different stages in their life cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle572_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle572-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Oceanic epipelagic fish can be true residents, partial residents, or accidental residents. True residents live their entire life in the open ocean. Only a few species are true residents, such as <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Billfish" title="Billfish">billfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flying_fish" title="Flying fish">flying fish</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sauries" title="Sauries">sauries</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pilot_fish" title="Pilot fish">pilotfish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Remora" title="Remora">remoras</a>, dolphin, ocean sharks and <a href="/wiki/Ocean_sunfish" title="Ocean sunfish">ocean sunfish</a>. Most of these species migrate back and forth across open oceans, rarely venturing over continental shelves. Some true residents associate with drifting jellyfish or seaweeds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle572_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle572-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p>Partial residents occur in three groups: species which live in the zone only when they are juveniles (drifting with jellyfish and seaweeds); species which live in the zone only when they are adults (salmon, flying fish, dolphin and whale sharks); and deep water species which make nightly migrations up into the surface waters (such as the <a href="/wiki/Lanternfish" title="Lanternfish">lanternfish</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle572_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle572-7">[7]</a></sup> Accidental residents occur occasionally when adults and juveniles of species from other environments are carried by accident into the zone by currents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle572_7-6"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle572-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 260px">
<div style="width: 260px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 255px;">
<div style="margin:15px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sunfish.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="280" data-file-width="200" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Sunfish.jpg/133px-Sunfish.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a2/Sunfish.jpg 1.5x" width="133"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The huge <a href="/wiki/Ocean_sunfish" title="Ocean sunfish">ocean sunfish</a>, a true resident of the ocean epipelagic zone, sometimes drifts with the current, eating <a href="/wiki/Jellyfish" title="Jellyfish">jellyfish</a></p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 260px">
<div style="width: 260px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 255px;">
<div style="margin:56.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Whale_shark_Georgia_aquarium.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1074" data-file-width="2331" height="104" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Whale_shark_Georgia_aquarium.jpg/225px-Whale_shark_Georgia_aquarium.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Whale_shark_Georgia_aquarium.jpg/338px-Whale_shark_Georgia_aquarium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Whale_shark_Georgia_aquarium.jpg/450px-Whale_shark_Georgia_aquarium.jpg 2x" width="225"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The giant <a href="/wiki/Whale_shark" title="Whale shark">whale shark</a>, another resident of the ocean epipelagic zone, filter feeds on <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a>, and periodically dives deep into the mesopelagic zone.</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 260px">
<div style="width: 260px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 255px;">
<div style="margin:61.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Protomyctophum_subparallelum_(no_common_name).gif"><img alt="" data-file-height="1055" data-file-width="2516" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Protomyctophum_subparallelum_%28no_common_name%29.gif/225px-Protomyctophum_subparallelum_%28no_common_name%29.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Protomyctophum_subparallelum_%28no_common_name%29.gif/338px-Protomyctophum_subparallelum_%28no_common_name%29.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Protomyctophum_subparallelum_%28no_common_name%29.gif/450px-Protomyctophum_subparallelum_%28no_common_name%29.gif 2x" width="225"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Lanternfish" title="Lanternfish">Lanternfish</a> are partial residents of the ocean epipelagic zone. During the day they hide in deep waters, but at night they migrate up to surface waters to feed.</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Deep_water_fish">Deep water fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelagic_fish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Deep water fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wfm_pelagic.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1686" data-file-width="576" height="468" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Wfm_pelagic.png/160px-Wfm_pelagic.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Wfm_pelagic.png/240px-Wfm_pelagic.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Wfm_pelagic.png/320px-Wfm_pelagic.png 2x" width="160"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wfm_pelagic.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Scale diagram of the layers of the pelagic zone</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Deep_sea_fish" title="Deep sea fish">Deep sea fish</a></div>
<p>In the deep ocean, the waters extend far below the epipelagic zone, and support very different types of pelagic fishes adapted to living in these deeper zones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle585_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle585-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>In deep water, <a href="/wiki/Marine_snow" title="Marine snow">marine snow</a> is a continuous shower of mostly organic <a href="/wiki/Detritus" title="Detritus">detritus</a> falling from the upper layers of the water column. Its origin lies in activities within the productive <a href="/wiki/Photic_zone" title="Photic zone">photic zone</a>. Marine snow includes dead or dying <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protists</a> (<a href="/wiki/Diatom" title="Diatom">diatoms</a>), fecal matter, sand, soot and other inorganic dust. The "snowflakes" grow over time and may reach several centimetres in diameter, travelling for weeks before reaching the ocean floor. However, most organic components of marine snow are consumed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microbe" title="Microbe">microbes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a> and other filter-feeding animals within the first 1,000 metres of their journey, that is, within the epipelagic zone. In this way marine snow may be considered the foundation of deep-sea <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesopelagic" title="Mesopelagic">mesopelagic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benthic" title="Benthic">benthic</a> <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>: As sunlight cannot reach them, deep-sea organisms rely heavily on marine snow as an energy source.</p>
<p>Some deep-sea pelagic groups, such as the <a href="/wiki/Lanternfish" title="Lanternfish">lanternfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ridgehead" title="Ridgehead">ridgehead</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marine_hatchetfish" title="Marine hatchetfish">marine hatchetfish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Phosichthyidae" title="Phosichthyidae">lightfish</a> families are sometimes termed <i>pseudoceanic</i> because, rather than having an even distribution in open water, they occur in significantly higher abundances around structural oases, notably <a href="/wiki/Seamount" title="Seamount">seamounts</a> and over <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_slope" title="Continental slope">continental slopes</a>. The phenomenon is explained by the likewise abundance of prey species which are also attracted to the structures.</p>
<p>The fish in the different pelagic and deep water benthic zones are physically structured, and behave in ways, that differ markedly from each other. Groups of coexisting species within each zone all seem to operate in similar ways, such as the small mesopelagic <a href="/wiki/Diel_vertical_migration" title="Diel vertical migration">vertically migrating</a> plankton-feeders, the bathypelagic <a href="/wiki/Anglerfish" title="Anglerfish">anglerfishes</a>, and the deep water benthic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rattail" title="Rattail">rattails</a>. "<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle591_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle591-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Acanthopterygii" title="Acanthopterygii">Ray finned</a> species, with spiny fins, are rare among deep sea fishes, which suggests that deep sea fish are ancient and so well adapted to their environment that invasions by more modern fishes have been unsuccessful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haedrich1996_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Haedrich1996-17">[17]</a></sup> The few ray fins that do exist are mainly in the <a href="/wiki/Beryciformes" title="Beryciformes">Beryciformes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lampriformes" title="Lampriformes">Lampriformes</a>, which are also ancient forms. Most deep sea pelagic fishes belong to their own orders, suggesting a long evolution in deep sea environments. In contrast, deep water benthic species, are in orders that include many related shallow water fishes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle586_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle586-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable collapsible collapsed">

</tr>

<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center; width:400px;">Many species move daily between zones in vertical migrations.<br/>
In this table they are listed in the middle or deeper zone where they are regularly found.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<th>Zone</th>
<th>Species and species groups include:</th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Epipelagic<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle571_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle571-5">[5]</a></sup></td>
<td>
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mackeral_shark" title="Mackeral shark">mackerel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Requiem_shark" title="Requiem shark">requiem</a> and <a href="/wiki/Whale_shark" title="Whale shark">whale sharks</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clupeiform" title="Clupeiform">clupeiforms</a> – <a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">herring</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anchovy" title="Anchovy">anchovy</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Salmonidae" title="Salmonidae">Salmonidae</a> – <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atheriniform" title="Atheriniform">atheriniforms</a> – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flyingfish" title="Flyingfish">flyingfishes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Halfbeak" title="Halfbeak">halfbeaks</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sauries" title="Sauries">sauries</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perciform" title="Perciform">perciforms</a> – <a href="/wiki/Carangidae" title="Carangidae">jacks</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dolphinfish" title="Dolphinfish">dolphinfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pomfret" title="Pomfret">pomfrets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barracuda" title="Barracuda">barracudas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tunas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Billfish" title="Billfish">billfish</a>.</li>
</ul>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>Mesopelagic</td>

</tr>
<tr>

Also <a href="/wiki/Fangtooth" title="Fangtooth">fangtooth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viperfish" title="Viperfish">viperfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_swallower" title="Black swallower">black swallower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Telescopefish" title="Telescopefish">telescopefish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hammerjaw" title="Hammerjaw">hammerjaw</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daggertooth" title="Daggertooth">daggertooth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barracudina" title="Barracudina">barracudina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Black_scabbardfish" title="Black scabbardfish">black scabbardfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bobtail_snipe_eel" title="Bobtail snipe eel">bobtail snipe eel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Unicorn_crestfish" title="Unicorn crestfish">unicorn crestfish</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulper_eel" title="Gulper eel">gulper eel</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flabby_whalefish" title="Flabby whalefish">flabby whalefish</a>.</td>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mesopelagic_fish">Mesopelagic fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelagic_fish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Mesopelagic fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:California_headlightfish.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="271" data-file-width="601" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/California_headlightfish.png/220px-California_headlightfish.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/California_headlightfish.png/330px-California_headlightfish.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/California_headlightfish.png/440px-California_headlightfish.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:California_headlightfish.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Most mesopelagic fishes are small filter feeders which ascend at night to feed in the nutrient rich waters of the epipelagic zone. During the day, they return to the dark, cold, oxygen deficient waters of the mesopelagic where they are relatively safe from predators. <a href="/wiki/Lanternfish" title="Lanternfish">Lanternfish</a> account for as much as 65% of all deep sea fish <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> and are largely responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Deep_scattering_layer" title="Deep scattering layer">deep scattering layer</a> of the world's oceans.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coccorella_atrata.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="218" data-file-width="628" height="76" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Coccorella_atrata.png/220px-Coccorella_atrata.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Coccorella_atrata.png/330px-Coccorella_atrata.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Coccorella_atrata.png/440px-Coccorella_atrata.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coccorella_atrata.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Most of the rest of the mesopelagic fishes are ambush predators, like this <a href="/wiki/Sabertooth_fish" title="Sabertooth fish">sabertooth fish</a>. The sabertooth which uses its telescopic, upward-pointing eyes to pick out prey silhouetted against the gloom above. Their recurved teeth prevent a captured fish from backing out.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Below the epipelagic zone, conditions change rapidly. Between 200 metres and about 1000 metres, light continues to fade until there is almost none. Temperatures fall through a <a href="/wiki/Thermocline" title="Thermocline">thermocline</a> to temperatures between 4 °C (39 °F) and 8 °C (46 °F). This is the <b>twilight</b> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mesopelagic" title="Mesopelagic">mesopelagic</a> zone. Pressure continues to increase, at the rate of one atmosphere every 10 metres, while nutrient concentrations fall, along with dissolved oxygen and the rate at which the water circulates."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle585_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle585-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sonar operators, using the newly developed sonar technology during World War II, were puzzled by what appeared to be a false sea floor 300–500 metres deep at day, and less deep at night. This turned out to be due to millions of marine organisms, most particularly small mesopelagic fish, with swimbladders that reflected the sonar. These organisms migrate up into shallower water at dusk to feed on plankton. The layer is deeper when the moon is out, and can become shallower when clouds pass over the moon. This phenomenon has come to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Deep_scattering_layer" title="Deep scattering layer">deep scattering layer</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TeAraMZ_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-TeAraMZ-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most mesopelagic fish make daily <a href="/wiki/Diel_vertical_migration" title="Diel vertical migration">vertical migrations</a>, moving at night into the epipelagic zone, often following similar migrations of zooplankton, and returning to the depths for safety during the day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle585_2-4"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle585-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> These vertical migrations often occur over a large vertical distances, and are undertaken with the assistance of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swimbladder" title="Swimbladder">swimbladder</a>. The swimbladder is inflated when the fish wants to move up, and, given the high pressures in the messoplegic zone, this requires significant energy. As the fish ascends, the pressure in the swimbladder must adjust to prevent it from bursting. When the fish wants to return to the depths, the swimbladder is deflated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Some mesopelagic fishes make daily migrations through the <a href="/wiki/Thermocline" title="Thermocline">thermocline</a>, where the temperature changes between 10 and 20 °C, thus displaying considerable tolerances for temperature change.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle590_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle590-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>These fish have muscular bodies, ossified bones, scales, well developed gills and central nervous systems, and large hearts and kidneys. Mesopelagic <a href="/wiki/Filter_feeder" title="Filter feeder">plankton feeders</a> have small mouths with fine <a href="/wiki/Gill_raker" title="Gill raker">gill rakers</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/Piscivore" title="Piscivore">piscivores</a> have larger mouths and coarser gill rakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle585_2-5"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle585-2">[2]</a></sup> The vertically migratory fish have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swimbladder" title="Swimbladder">swimbladders</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haedrich1996_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Haedrich1996-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mesopelagic fish are adapted for an active life under low light conditions. Most of them are visual predators with large eyes. Some of the deeper water fish have tubular eyes with big lenses and only <a href="/wiki/Rod_cell" title="Rod cell">rod cells</a> that look upwards. These give binocular vision and great sensitivity to small light signals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle585_2-6"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle585-2">[2]</a></sup> This adaptation gives improved terminal vision at the expense of lateral vision, and allows the predator to pick out <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuttlefish" title="Cuttlefish">cuttlefish</a>, and smaller fish that are silhouetted against the gloom above them.</p>
<p>Mesopelagic fish usually lack defensive spines, and use colour to <a href="/wiki/Camouflage" title="Camouflage">camouflage</a> them from other fish. <a href="/wiki/Ambush_predator" title="Ambush predator">Ambush predators</a> are dark, black or red. Since the longer, red, wavelengths of light do not reach the deep sea, red effectively functions the same as black. Migratory forms use <a href="/wiki/Countershading" title="Countershading">countershaded</a> silvery colours. On their bellies, they often display <a href="/wiki/Photophore" title="Photophore">photophores</a> producing low grade light. For a predator from below, looking upwards, this <a href="/wiki/Bioluminescence" title="Bioluminescence">bioluminescence</a> camouflages the silhouette of the fish. However, some of these predators have yellow lenses that filter the (red deficient) ambient light, leaving the bioluminescence visible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">
<div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="margin:27.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dmawsoni_Head_shot.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="320" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Dmawsoni_Head_shot.jpg/200px-Dmawsoni_Head_shot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/Dmawsoni_Head_shot.jpg/300px-Dmawsoni_Head_shot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9d/Dmawsoni_Head_shot.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_toothfish" title="Antarctic toothfish">Antarctic toothfish</a> have large, upward looking eyes, adapted to detecting the silhouettes of prey fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">
<div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="margin:63.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Opisthoproctus_soleatus.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="242" data-file-width="617" height="78" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Opisthoproctus_soleatus.png/200px-Opisthoproctus_soleatus.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Opisthoproctus_soleatus.png/300px-Opisthoproctus_soleatus.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Opisthoproctus_soleatus.png/400px-Opisthoproctus_soleatus.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Barreleye" title="Barreleye">Barreleye</a> has barrel-shaped, tubular <a href="/wiki/Eye" title="Eye">eyes</a> which are generally directed upwards but can be swivelled forward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">
<div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="margin:67.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Malacosteus_niger.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="418" data-file-width="1188" height="70" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Malacosteus_niger.jpg/200px-Malacosteus_niger.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Malacosteus_niger.jpg/300px-Malacosteus_niger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Malacosteus_niger.jpg/400px-Malacosteus_niger.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Stoplight_loosejaw" title="Stoplight loosejaw">stoplight loosejaw</a> has a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_jaw" title="Lower jaw">lower jaw</a> one-quarter as long as its body. The jaw has no floor and is attached only by a hinge and a modified tongue bone. Large fang-like teeth in the front are followed by many small barbed teeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kenaley_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-kenaley-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sutton_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-sutton-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">
<div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="margin:35.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Malacosteus.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="544" data-file-width="811" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Malacosteus.JPG/200px-Malacosteus.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Malacosteus.JPG/300px-Malacosteus.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Malacosteus.JPG/400px-Malacosteus.JPG 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Stoplight_loosejaw" title="Stoplight loosejaw">stoplight loosejaw</a> is also one of the few fishes that produce red <a href="/wiki/Bioluminescence" title="Bioluminescence">bioluminescence</a>. As most of their prey cannot perceive red light, this allows it to hunt with an essentially invisible beam of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kenaley_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-kenaley-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Brownsnout_spookfish" title="Brownsnout spookfish">brownsnout spookfish</a> is a species of <a href="/wiki/Barreleye" title="Barreleye">barreleye</a> is the only vertebrate known to employ a mirror, as opposed to a lens, to focus an image in its eyes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wagner_et_al_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-wagner_et_al-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smith_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-smith-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sampling via deep <a href="/wiki/Trawling" title="Trawling">trawling</a> indicates that <a href="/wiki/Lanternfish" title="Lanternfish">lanternfish</a> account for as much as 65% of all deep sea fish <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EoF_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-EoF-31">[31]</a></sup> Indeed, lanternfish are among the most widely distributed, populous, and diverse of all <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrates</a>, playing an important <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecological</a> role as prey for larger organisms. The estimated global biomass of lanternfish is 550–660 million <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metric_tonne" title="Metric tonne">metric tonnes</a>, several times the entire world fisheries catch. Lanternfish also account for much of the biomass responsible for the <a href="/wiki/Deep_scattering_layer" title="Deep scattering layer">deep scattering layer</a> of the world's oceans. <a href="/wiki/Sonar" title="Sonar">Sonar</a> reflects off the millions of lanternfish <a href="/wiki/Swim_bladder" title="Swim bladder">swim bladders</a>, giving the appearance of a false bottom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bigeye_tuna" title="Bigeye tuna">Bigeye tuna</a> are an epipelagic/mesopelagic species that eats other fish. Satellite tagging has shown that bigeye tuna often spend prolonged periods cruising deep below the surface during the daytime, sometimes making dives as deep as 500 metres. These movements are thought to be in response to the vertical migrations of prey organisms in the <a href="/wiki/Deep_scattering_layer" title="Deep scattering layer">deep scattering layer</a>.</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">
<div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="margin:55.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Longnoselancetfish.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="188" data-file-width="400" height="94" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Longnoselancetfish.jpg/200px-Longnoselancetfish.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Longnoselancetfish.jpg/300px-Longnoselancetfish.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0d/Longnoselancetfish.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Longnose_lancetfish" title="Longnose lancetfish">Longnose lancetfish</a>. Lancetfish are ambush predators which spend all their time in the mesopelagic zone. They are among the largest mesopelagic fishes (up to 2 metres).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle336_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle336-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">
<div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="margin:78px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gigantura_chuni.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="385" data-file-width="1565" height="49" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Gigantura_chuni.png/200px-Gigantura_chuni.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Gigantura_chuni.png/300px-Gigantura_chuni.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Gigantura_chuni.png/400px-Gigantura_chuni.png 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Telescopefish" title="Telescopefish">telescopefish</a> has large, forward-pointing telescoping eyes with large lenses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">
<div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="margin:58.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Daggertooth.PNG"><img alt="" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="944" height="88" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Daggertooth.PNG/200px-Daggertooth.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Daggertooth.PNG/300px-Daggertooth.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Daggertooth.PNG/400px-Daggertooth.PNG 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daggertooth" title="Daggertooth">daggertooth</a> paralyses other mesopelagic fish when it bites them with its dagger-like teeth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">

<p><a href="/wiki/Bigeye_tuna" title="Bigeye tuna">Bigeye tuna</a> cruise the epipelagic zone at night and the mesopelagic zone during the day.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bathypelagic_fish">Bathypelagic fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelagic_fish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Bathypelagic fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Humpback_anglerfish.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="473" data-file-width="505" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Humpback_anglerfish.png/220px-Humpback_anglerfish.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Humpback_anglerfish.png/330px-Humpback_anglerfish.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Humpback_anglerfish.png/440px-Humpback_anglerfish.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Humpback_anglerfish.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Humpback_anglerfish" title="Humpback anglerfish">humpback anglerfish</a> is a bathypelagic ambush predator, which attracts prey with a bioluminescent lure. It can ingest prey larger than itself, which it swallows with an inrush of water when it opens its mouth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TeAraBZ_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-TeAraBZ-36">[36]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gonostoma_bathyphilum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="1175" height="62" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Gonostoma_bathyphilum.jpg/220px-Gonostoma_bathyphilum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Gonostoma_bathyphilum.jpg/330px-Gonostoma_bathyphilum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Gonostoma_bathyphilum.jpg/440px-Gonostoma_bathyphilum.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gonostoma_bathyphilum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bristlemouth" title="Bristlemouth">bristlemouth</a> species, such as the "spark anglemouth" above,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> are also bathypelagic ambush predators which can swallow prey larger than themselves. They are among the most abundant of all vertebrate families.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flabby_whalefish.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="278" data-file-width="1280" height="48" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Flabby_whalefish.jpg/220px-Flabby_whalefish.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Flabby_whalefish.jpg/330px-Flabby_whalefish.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Flabby_whalefish.jpg/440px-Flabby_whalefish.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flabby_whalefish.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Young, red <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flabby_whalefish" title="Flabby whalefish">flabby whalefish</a> make nightly vertical migrations into the lower mesopelagic zone to feed on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copepods" title="Copepods">copepods</a>. When males make the transition to adults, they develop a massive liver, and then their jaws fuse shut. They no longer eat, but continue to metabolise the energy stored in their liver.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aparticle_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-aparticle-39">[39]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Below the mesopelagic zone it is pitch dark. This is the <b>midnight</b> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bathypelagic_zone" title="Bathypelagic zone">bathypelagic zone</a>, extending from 1000 m to the bottom deep water <a href="/wiki/Benthic_zone" title="Benthic zone">benthic zone</a>. If the water is exceptionally deep, the pelagic zone below 4000 m is sometimes called the <b>lower midnight</b> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abyssopelagic_zone" title="Abyssopelagic zone">abyssopelagic zone</a>.</p>
<p>Conditions are somewhat uniform throughout these zones, the darkness is complete, the pressure is crushing, and temperatures, nutrients and dissolved oxygen levels are all low.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle585_2-7"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle585-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bathypelagic fish have special <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adaptations</a> to cope with these conditions – they have slow <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolisms</a> and unspecialized diets, being willing to eat anything that comes along. They prefer to sit and wait for food rather than waste energy searching for it. The behaviour of bathypelagic fish can be contrasted with the behaviour of mesopelagic fish. Mesopelagic are often highly mobile, whereas bathypelagic fish are almost all lie-in-wait predators, normally expending little energy in movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle594_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle594-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>The dominant bathypelagic fishes are small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bristlemouth" title="Bristlemouth">bristlemouth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglerfish" title="Anglerfish">anglerfish</a>; <a href="/wiki/Fangtooth" title="Fangtooth">fangtooth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viperfish" title="Viperfish">viperfish</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daggertooth" title="Daggertooth">daggertooth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Barracudina" title="Barracudina">barracudina</a> are also common. These fishes are small, many about 10 centimetres long, and not many longer than 25 cm. They spend most of their time waiting patiently in the water column for prey to appear or to be lured by their phosphors. What little energy is available in the bathypelagic zone filters from above in the form of detritus, faecal material, and the occasional invertebrate or mesopelagic fish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle594_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle594-40">[40]</a></sup> About 20% of the food that has its origins in the epipelagic zone falls down to the mesopelagic zone,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TeAraMZ_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-TeAraMZ-20">[20]</a></sup> but only about 5% filters down to the bathypelagic zone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TeAraBZ_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-TeAraBZ-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bathypelagic fish are sedentary, adapted to outputting minimum energy in a habitat with very little food or available energy, not even sunlight, only bioluminescence. Their bodies are <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/elongate" title="wiktionary:elongate">elongated</a> with weak, watery muscles and <a href="/wiki/Skeleton" title="Skeleton">skeletal</a> structures. Since so much of the fish is water, they are not compressed by the great pressures at these depths. They often have extensible, hinged <a href="/wiki/Jaw#The_jaw_in_fish" title="Jaw">jaws</a> with recurved teeth. They are slimy, without <a href="/wiki/Fish_scale" title="Fish scale">scales</a>. The central nervous system is confined to the lateral line and olfactory systems, the eyes are small and may not function, and <a href="/wiki/Gill" title="Gill">gills</a>, kidneys and hearts, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swimbladder" title="Swimbladder">swimbladders</a> are small or missing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TeAraBZ_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-TeAraBZ-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle587_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle587-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>These are the same features found in fish <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Larvae" title="Larvae">larvae</a>, which suggests that during their evolution, bathypelagic fish have acquired these features through <a href="/wiki/Neoteny" title="Neoteny">neoteny</a>. As with larvae, these features allow the fish to remain suspended in the water with little expenditure of energy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite their ferocious appearance, these beasts of the deep are mostly miniature fish with weak muscles, and are too small to represent any threat to humans.</p>
<p>The swimbladders of deep sea fish are either absent or scarcely operational, and bathypelagic fish do not normally undertake vertical migrations. Filling bladders at such great pressures incurs huge energy costs. Some deep sea fishes have swimbladders which function while they are young and inhabit the upper epipelagic zone, but they wither or fill with fat when the fish move down to their adult habitat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>The most important sensory systems are usually the <a href="/wiki/Inner_ear" title="Inner ear">inner ear</a>, which responds to sound, and the <a href="/wiki/Lateral_line" title="Lateral line">lateral line</a>, which responds to changes in water pressure. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olfactory" title="Olfactory">olfactory</a> system can also be important for males who find females by smell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Bathypelagic fish are black, or sometimes red, with few <a href="/wiki/Photophore" title="Photophore">photophores</a>. When photophores are used, it is usually to entice prey or attract a mate. Because food is so scarce, bathypelagic predators are not selective in their feeding habits, but grab whatever come close enough. They accomplish this by having a large mouth with sharp teeth for grabbing large prey and overlapping <a href="/wiki/Gill_raker" title="Gill raker">gill rakers</a> which prevent small prey that have been swallowed from escaping.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle587_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle587-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is not easy finding a mate in this zone. Some species depend on <a href="/wiki/Bioluminescence" title="Bioluminescence">bioluminescence</a>. Others are <a href="/wiki/Hermaphrodite" title="Hermaphrodite">hermaphrodites</a>, which doubles their chances of producing both eggs and sperm when an encounter occurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TeAraBZ_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-TeAraBZ-36">[36]</a></sup> The female anglerfish releases <a href="/wiki/Pheromone" title="Pheromone">pheromones</a> to attract tiny males. When a male finds her, he bites on to her and never lets go. When a male of the anglerfish species <i><a href="/wiki/Haplophryne_mollis" title="Haplophryne mollis">Haplophryne mollis</a></i> bites into the skin of a female, he release an <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> that digests the skin of his mouth and her body, fusing the pair to the point where the two circulatory systems join up. The male then atrophies into nothing more than a pair of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gonads" title="Gonads">gonads</a>. This extreme <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">sexual dimorphism</a> ensures that, when the female is ready to spawn, she has a mate immediately available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doran_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-doran-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many forms other than fish live in the bathypelagic zone, such as squid, large whales, octopuses, sponges, <a href="/wiki/Brachiopod" title="Brachiopod">brachiopods</a>, sea stars, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Echinoid" title="Echinoid">echinoids</a>, but this zone is difficult for fish to live in.</p>

<p>The widespread <a href="/wiki/Fangtooth" title="Fangtooth">fangtooth</a> has the largest teeth of any fish, proportionate to body size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Despite their ferocious appearance, bathypelagic fish are usually weakly muscled and too small to represent any threat to humans.</p>
