<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demersal_fish">Demersal fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelagic_fish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Demersal fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Giant_grenadier.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="504" data-file-width="496" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Giant_grenadier.jpg/220px-Giant_grenadier.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Giant_grenadier.jpg/330px-Giant_grenadier.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Giant_grenadier.jpg/440px-Giant_grenadier.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Giant_grenadier.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Giant_grenadier" title="Giant grenadier">Giant grenadier</a>, an <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/elongate" title="wiktionary:elongate">elongate</a> benthic fish with large eyes and well-developed <a href="/wiki/Lateral_line" title="Lateral line">lateral lines</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Demersal_fish" title="Demersal fish">Demersal fish</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Demersal_fish" title="Demersal fish">Demersal fish</a> live on or near the bottom of the sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seafloor_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-seafloor-50">[50]</a></sup> Demersal fish are found by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seafloor" title="Seafloor">seafloor</a> in coastal areas on the <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a>, and in the open ocean they are found along the outer <a href="/wiki/Continental_margin" title="Continental margin">continental margin</a> on the continental slope and the continental rise. They are not generally found at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abyssopelagic" title="Abyssopelagic">abyssopelagic</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hadopelagic" title="Hadopelagic">hadopelagic</a> depths or on the <a href="/wiki/Abyssal_plain" title="Abyssal plain">abyssal plain</a>. They occupy a range of seafloors consisting of mud, sand, gravel or rocks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seafloor_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-seafloor-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>In deep waters, the fishes of the demersal zone are active and relatively abundant, compared to fishes of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bathypelagic_zone" title="Bathypelagic zone">bathypelagic zone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle594_40-2"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle594-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rattail" title="Rattail">Rattails</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brotula" title="Brotula">brotulas</a> are common, and other well-established families are <a href="/wiki/Eel" title="Eel">eels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eelpout" title="Eelpout">eelpouts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hagfish" title="Hagfish">hagfishes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greeneye" title="Greeneye">greeneyes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pancake_batfish" title="Pancake batfish">batfishes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lumpfish" title="Lumpfish">lumpfishes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle587_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle587-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>The bodies of deep water <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benthic" title="Benthic">benthic</a> fishes are muscular with well developed organs. In this way they are closer to mesopelagic fishes than bathopelagic fishes. In other ways, they are more variable. <a href="/wiki/Photophore" title="Photophore">Photophores</a> are usually absent, eyes and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swimbladder" title="Swimbladder">swimbladders</a> range from absent to well developed. They vary in size, with larger species greater than one metre not uncommon.</p>
<p>Deep sea benthic fish are usually long and narrow. Many are eels or shaped like eels. This may be because long bodies have long <a href="/wiki/Lateral_line" title="Lateral line">lateral lines</a>. Lateral lines detect low-frequency sounds, and some benthic fishes appear to have muscles that drum such sounds to attract mates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Haedrich1996_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Haedrich1996-17">[17]</a></sup> Smell is also important, as indicated by the rapidity with which benthic fish find traps baited with <a href="/wiki/Bait_fish" title="Bait fish">bait fish</a>.</p>
<p>The main diet of deep sea benthic fish is invertebrates of the deep sea <a href="/wiki/Benthos" title="Benthos">benthos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carrion</a>. Smell, touch and lateral line sensitivities seem to be the main sensory devices for locating these.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle588_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle588-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Deep sea benthic fish can be divided into strictly benthic fish and benthopelagic fish. Usually, strictly benthic fish are negatively buoyant, while benthopelagic fish are neutrally buoyant. Strictly benthic fish stay in constant contact with the bottom. They either lie-and-wait as <a href="/wiki/Ambush_predator" title="Ambush predator">ambush predators</a> or move actively over the bottom in search for food.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle588_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle588-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Orange_roughy.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="289" data-file-width="577" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Orange_roughy.png/220px-Orange_roughy.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Orange_roughy.png/330px-Orange_roughy.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Orange_roughy.png/440px-Orange_roughy.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Orange_roughy.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Orange_roughy" title="Orange roughy">Orange roughy</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Toothfish.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="561" data-file-width="1585" height="78" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Toothfish.jpg/220px-Toothfish.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Toothfish.jpg/330px-Toothfish.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Toothfish.jpg/440px-Toothfish.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Toothfish.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Patagonian_toothfish" title="Patagonian toothfish">Patagonian toothfish</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Benthopelagic_fish">Benthopelagic fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelagic_fish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Benthopelagic fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benthopelagic_fish" title="Benthopelagic fish">Benthopelagic fish</a></div>
<p>Benthopelagic fish inhabit the water just above the bottom, feeding on <a href="/wiki/Benthos" title="Benthos">benthos</a> and benthopelagic <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Most dermersal fish are benthopelagic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seafloor_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-seafloor-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>They can be divided into flabby or robust body types. Flabby benthopelagic fishes are like bathopelagic fishes, they have a reduced body mass, and low metabolic rates, expending minimal energy as they lie and wait to <a href="/wiki/Ambush_predator" title="Ambush predator">ambush</a> prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> An example of a flabby fish is the cusk-eel <i><a href="/wiki/Acanthonus_armatus" title="Acanthonus armatus">Acanthonus armatus</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fishbase_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-fishbase-54">[54]</a></sup> a predator with a huge head and a body that is 90% water. This fish has the largest ears (<a href="/wiki/Otolith" title="Otolith">otoliths</a>) and the smallest brain in relation to its body size of all known vertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fine_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fine-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>Robust benthopelagic fish are muscular swimmers that actively cruise the bottom searching for prey. They may live around features, such as <a href="/wiki/Seamount" title="Seamount">seamounts</a>, which have strong currents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fine_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fine-55">[55]</a></sup> Examples are the <a href="/wiki/Orange_roughy" title="Orange roughy">orange roughy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patagonian_toothfish" title="Patagonian toothfish">Patagonian toothfish</a>. Because these fish were once abundant, and because their robust bodies are good to eat, these fish have been commercially harvested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Benthic_fish">Benthic fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelagic_fish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Benthic fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benthic_fish" title="Benthic fish">Benthic fish</a></div>
<p>Benthic fish are not pelagic fish, but they are discussed here briefly, by way of completeness and contrast.</p>
<p>Some fishes don't fit into the above classification. For example, the family of nearly blind <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiderfish" title="Spiderfish">spiderfishes</a>, common and widely distributed, feed on benthopelagic zooplankton. Yet they are strictly benthic fish, since they stay in contact with the bottom. Their fins have long rays they use to "stand" on the bottom while they face the current and grab zooplankton as it passes by.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>The deepest-living fish known, the strictly benthic <i><a href="/wiki/Abyssobrotula_galatheae" title="Abyssobrotula galatheae">Abyssobrotula galatheae</a></i>, eel-like and blind, feeds on benthic invertebrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">
<div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="margin:27.5px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pacific_hagfish_Myxine.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="149" data-file-width="199" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/12/Pacific_hagfish_Myxine.jpg" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p><a href="/wiki/Pacific_hagfish" title="Pacific hagfish">Pacific hagfish</a> resting on bottom. Hagfish coat themselves and any dead fish they find with noxious slime making them inedible to other species."</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">
<div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="margin:60px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bathypterois_grallator.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="490" data-file-width="1150" height="85" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Bathypterois_grallator.jpg/200px-Bathypterois_grallator.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Bathypterois_grallator.jpg/300px-Bathypterois_grallator.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Bathypterois_grallator.jpg/400px-Bathypterois_grallator.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The tripodfish (<i><a href="/wiki/Bathypterois_grallator" title="Bathypterois grallator">Bathypterois grallator</a></i>), a species of spiderfish, uses its fin extensions to "stand" on the bottom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">
<div style="width: 235px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 230px;">
<div style="margin:36px auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taeniura_meyeni_reef.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Taeniura_meyeni_reef.jpg/200px-Taeniura_meyeni_reef.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Taeniura_meyeni_reef.jpg/300px-Taeniura_meyeni_reef.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Taeniura_meyeni_reef.jpg/400px-Taeniura_meyeni_reef.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blotched_fantail_ray" title="Blotched fantail ray">blotched fantail ray</a> feeds on bottom-dwelling fish, bivalves, crabs and shrimps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oceanic_basin.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="705" data-file-width="1001" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Oceanic_basin.svg/220px-Oceanic_basin.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Oceanic_basin.svg/330px-Oceanic_basin.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Oceanic_basin.svg/440px-Oceanic_basin.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oceanic_basin.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cross-section of an ocean basin. Note significant <a href="/wiki/Vertical_exaggeration" title="Vertical exaggeration">vertical exaggeration</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At great depths, food scarcity and extreme pressure works to limit the survivability fish. The deepest point of the ocean is about 11,000 metres. Bathypelagic fishes are not normally found below 3,000 metres. The greatest depth recorded for a benthic fish is 8,370 m.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nielsen_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-nielsen-63">[63]</a></sup> It may be that extreme pressures interfere with essential enzyme functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TeAraBZ_36-4"><a href="#cite_note-TeAraBZ-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Benthic fishes are likely to be found, and are more diverse, on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_slope" title="Continental slope">continental slope</a>, where there is habitat diversity and often food supplies. About 40% of the ocean floor consists of <a href="/wiki/Abyssal_plain" title="Abyssal plain">abyssal plains</a>, but these flat, featureless regions are covered with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelagic_sediments" title="Pelagic sediments">sediment</a> and largely devoid of benthic life (<a href="/wiki/Benthos" title="Benthos">benthos</a>). Deep sea benthic fishes are more likely to associate with canyons or rock outcroppings among the plains, where invertebrate communities are established. Undersea mountains (<a href="/wiki/Seamount" title="Seamount">seamounts</a>) can intercept deep sea currents, and cause productive upwellings which support benthic fish. Undersea mountain ranges can separate underwater regions into different ecosystems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle591_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle591-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pelagic_fisheries">Pelagic fisheries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelagic_fish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Pelagic fisheries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<tr>
<td style="text-align: left; font-size: 88%; border: 0; padding: 0;">Important marine wild fisheries</td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Forage_fish" title="Forage fish">Forage fish</a></div>
<p>Small pelagic fish are usually <a href="/wiki/Forage_fish" title="Forage fish">forage fish</a> that are hunted by larger pelagic fish and other predators. Forage fish <a href="/wiki/Filter_feeder" title="Filter feeder">filter feed</a> on <a href="/wiki/Plankton" title="Plankton">plankton</a> and are usually less than 10 centimetres long. They often stay together in <a href="/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling" title="Shoaling and schooling">schools</a> and may <a href="/wiki/Fish_migration" title="Fish migration">migrate</a> large distances between spawning grounds and feeding grounds. They are found particularly in <a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">upwelling</a> regions around the northeast Atlantic, off the coast of Japan, and off the west coasts of Africa and the Americas. Forage fish are generally short-lived, and their <a href="/wiki/Fish_stock" title="Fish stock">stocks</a> fluctuate markedly over the years. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Checkley_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Checkley-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Herring" title="Herring">Herring</a> are found in the <a href="/wiki/North_Sea" title="North Sea">North Sea</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Atlantic" title="North Atlantic">North Atlantic</a> at depths to 200 meters. Important herring fisheries have existed in these areas for centuries. Herring of different sizes and growth rates belong to different populations, each of which have their own migration routes. When spawning, a female produces from 20,000 to 50,000 eggs. After spawning, the herrings are depleted in fat, and migrate back to feeding grounds rich in plankton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pfa_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-pfa-65">[65]</a></sup> Around Iceland, three separate populations of herring were traditionally fished. These stocks collapsed in the late 1960s, although two have since recovered. After the collapse, Iceland turned to <a href="/wiki/Capelin" title="Capelin">capelin</a>, which now account for about half of Iceland's total catch.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Blue_whiting" title="Blue whiting">Blue whiting</a> are found in the open ocean and above the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continental_slope" title="Continental slope">continental slope</a> at depths between 100 and 1000 meters. They follow vertical migrations of the <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a> they feed on to the bottom during daytime and to the surface at night time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pfa_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-pfa-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>Traditional fisheries for <a href="/wiki/Anchovy" title="Anchovy">anchovies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sardine" title="Sardine">sardines</a> have also operated in the Pacific, the Mediterranean, and the southeast Atlantic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bone443_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bone443-68">[68]</a></sup> The world annual catch of forage fish in recent years has been around 22 million tonnes, or one quarter of the world's total catch.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Predator_fish">Predator fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelagic_fish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Predator fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator_fish" title="Predator fish">Predator fish</a></div>
<p>Medium size pelagic fishes include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trevally" title="Trevally">trevally</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barracuda" title="Barracuda">barracuda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flying_fish" title="Flying fish">flying fish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bonito" title="Bonito">bonito</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahi_mahi" title="Mahi mahi">mahi mahi</a> and coastal mackerel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lal8_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-Lal8-1">[1]</a></sup> Many of these fish hunt forage fish, but are in turn hunted by yet larger pelagic fish. Nearly all fish are predator fish to some measure, and apart from the top predators, the distinction between predator fish and prey or forage fish is somewhat artificial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Around Europe there are three populations of coastal <a href="/wiki/Mackerel" title="Mackerel">mackerel</a>. One population migrates to the North Sea, another stays in of the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sea" title="Irish Sea">Irish Sea</a>, and the third population migrates southwards along the west coast of Scotland and Ireland. The mackerel's cruise speed is an impressive 10 kilometres per hour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pfa_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-pfa-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mackerel_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mackerel-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many large pelagic fish are oceanic nomadic species which undertake long offshore migrations. They feed on small pelagic forage fish, as well as medium-sized pelagic fish. At times, they follow their schooling prey, and many species form schools themselves.</p>
<p>Examples of larger pelagic fish are <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Billfish" title="Billfish">billfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/King_mackerel" title="King mackerel">king mackerel</a> and sharks and large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ray-finned_fish" title="Ray-finned fish">rays</a>.</p>
<p>Tuna in particular are of major importance to commercial fisheries. Though tuna migrate across oceans, trying to find them there is not the usual approach. Tuna tend to congregate in areas where food is abundant, along the boundaries of currents, around islands, near seamounts, and in some areas of upwelling along continental slopes. Tuna are captured by several methods: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seiner" title="Seiner">purse seine vessels</a> enclose an entire surface school with special nets, <a href="/wiki/Fishing_vessel#Line_vessels" title="Fishing vessel">pole and line vessels</a> which use poles baited with other smaller pelagic fish as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baitfish" title="Baitfish">baitfish</a>, and rafts called <a href="/wiki/Fish_aggregating_device" title="Fish aggregating device">fish aggregating devices</a> are set up, because tuna, as well as some other pelagic fish, tend to congregate under floating objects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lal8_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-Lal8-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other large pelagic fish are premier <a href="/wiki/Game_fish" title="Game fish">game fish</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Marlin" title="Marlin">marlin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swordfish" title="Swordfish">swordfish</a>.</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 222px">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Productivity">Productivity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelagic_fish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Productivity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Upwelling" title="Upwelling">Upwelling</a> occurs both along coastlines and in midocean when a collision of deep <a href="/wiki/Ocean_current" title="Ocean current">ocean currents</a> brings cold water rich in nutrients to the surface. These upwellings support blooms of phytoplankton, which in turn produce zooplankton and support many of the world's main fisheries. If the upwelling fails then fisheries in the area fail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle574.2F5_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle574.2F5-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1960s the <a href="/wiki/Peruvian_anchoveta" title="Peruvian anchoveta">Peruvian anchoveta</a> fishery was the world's largest fishery. The anchoveta population was greatly reduced during the 1972 <a href="/wiki/El_Ni%C3%B1o" title="El Niño">El Niño</a> event, when warm water drifted over the cold <a href="/wiki/Humboldt_Current" title="Humboldt Current">Humboldt Current</a>, as part of a 50-year cycle, lowering the depth of the <a href="/wiki/Thermocline" title="Thermocline">thermocline</a>. The upwelling stopped and <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a> production plummeted, as did the anchoveta population, and millions of <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">seabirds</a>, dependent on the anchoveta, died.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Since the mid-1980s, the upwelling has resumed, and the Peruvian anchoveta catch levels have returned to the 1960s levels.</p>
<p>Off Japan, the collision of the <a href="/wiki/Oyashio_Current" title="Oyashio Current">Oyashio Current</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Kuroshio_Current" title="Kuroshio Current">Kuroshio Current</a> produces nutrient-rich upwellings. Cyclic changes in these currents resulted in a decline in the <a href="/wiki/Sardine" title="Sardine">sardine</a> <i>sardinops melanosticta</i> populations. Fisheries catches fell from 5 million tonnes in 1988 to 280 thousand tonnes in 1998. As a further consequence, <a href="/wiki/Pacific_bluefin_tuna" title="Pacific bluefin tuna">Pacific bluefin tuna</a> stopped moving into the region to feed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ocean currents can shape how fish are distributed, both concentrating and dispersing them. Adjacent ocean currents can define distinct, if shifting, boundaries. These boundaries can even be visible, but usually their presence is marked by rapid changes in salinity, temperature and turbidity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle574.2F5_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle574.2F5-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>For example, in the Asian northern Pacific, <a href="/wiki/Albacore" title="Albacore">albacore</a> are confined between two current systems. The northern boundary is determined by the cold <a href="/wiki/North_Pacific_Current" title="North Pacific Current">North Pacific Current</a> and the southern boundary is determined by the <a href="/wiki/North_Equatorial_Current" title="North Equatorial Current">North Equatorial Current</a>. To complicate things, their distribution is further modified within the area defined by the two current systems by another current, the <a href="/wiki/Kuroshio_Current" title="Kuroshio Current">Kuroshio Current</a>, whose flows fluctuate seasonally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Epipelagic fish often <a href="/wiki/Spawn_(biology)" title="Spawn (biology)">spawn</a> in an area where the eggs and larvae drift downstream into suitable feeding areas, and eventually drift into adult feeding areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle574.2F5_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle574.2F5-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Islands and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fishing_bank" title="Fishing bank">banks</a> can interact with currents and upwellings in a manner that results in areas of high ocean productivity. Large eddies can form downcurrent or downwind from islands, concentrating plankton.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Banks and reefs can intercept deep currents that upwell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle574.2F5_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle574.2F5-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scombrid" title="Scombrid">Scombrids</a></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Highly_migratory_species">Highly migratory species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelagic_fish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Highly migratory species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Highly_migratory_species" title="Highly migratory species">Highly migratory species</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Isurus-oxyrinchus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="208" data-file-width="412" height="111" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Isurus-oxyrinchus.jpg/220px-Isurus-oxyrinchus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Isurus-oxyrinchus.jpg/330px-Isurus-oxyrinchus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e3/Isurus-oxyrinchus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Isurus-oxyrinchus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Shortfin_mako_shark" title="Shortfin mako shark">Shortfin mako shark</a> make long seasonal migrations. They appear to follow temperature gradients, and have been recorded travelling more than 4,500 km in one year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Epipelagic fish generally move long distances between feeding and spawning areas, or as a response to changes in the ocean. Large ocean predators, such as salmon and tuna, can migrate thousands of kilometres, crossing oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moyle578_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moyle578-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a 2001 study, the movements of <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_bluefin_tuna" title="Atlantic bluefin tuna">Atlantic bluefin tuna</a> from an area off North Carolina were studied with the help of special popup tags. When attached to a tuna, these tags monitored the movements of the tuna for about a year, then freed themselves, and floated to the surface where they transmitted their information to a satellite. The study found that the tuna had four different migration patterns. One group confined itself to the western Atlantic for a year. Another group also stayed mainly in the western Atlantic, but migrated to the Gulf of Mexico for spawning. A third group moved across the Atlantic Ocean and back again. The fourth group crossed to the eastern Atlantic and then moved into the Mediterranean Sea for spawning. The study indicates that, while there is some differentiation by spawning areas, there is essentially only one population of Atlantic bluefin tuna, intermixing groups that between them use all of the north Atlantic Ocean, the Gulf of Mexico and the Mediterranean Sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<p>The term <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Highly_migratory_species" title="Highly migratory species">highly migratory species</a> (HMS) is a legal term which has its origins in Article 64 of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Convention_on_the_Law_of_the_Sea" title="United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea">United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea</a> (UNCLOS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>The highly migratory species include: <a href="/wiki/Tuna" title="Tuna">tuna</a> and tuna-like species (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albacore_tuna" title="Albacore tuna">albacore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_bluefin_tuna" title="Atlantic bluefin tuna">Atlantic bluefin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bigeye_tuna" title="Bigeye tuna">bigeye tuna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Skipjack_tuna" title="Skipjack tuna">skipjack</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yellowfin_tuna" title="Yellowfin tuna">yellowfin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blackfin_tuna" title="Blackfin tuna">blackfin</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euthynnus_alletteratus" title="Euthynnus alletteratus">little tunny</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pacific_bluefin_tuna" title="Pacific bluefin tuna">Pacific bluefin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southern_bluefin_tuna" title="Southern bluefin tuna">southern bluefin</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auxis_rochei" title="Auxis rochei">bullet</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bramidae" title="Bramidae">pomfret</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marlin" title="Marlin">marlin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sailfish" title="Sailfish">sailfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swordfish" title="Swordfish">swordfish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saury" title="Saury">saury</a> and oceangoing <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dolphin" title="Dolphin">dolphins</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cetacean" title="Cetacean">cetaceans</a>.</p>
<p>Essentially, highly migratory species coincide with the larger of the "large pelagic fish", discussed in the previous section, if cetaceans are added and some commercially unimportant fish, such as the <a href="/wiki/Molidae" title="Molidae">sunfish</a>, are excluded. These are high <a href="/wiki/Trophic_level" title="Trophic level">trophic level</a> species which undertake migrations of significant but variable distances across oceans for feeding, often on forage fish, or reproduction, and also have wide geographic distributions. Thus, these species are found both inside the 200-nautical-mile (370 km) <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_economic_zone" title="Exclusive economic zone">exclusive economic zones</a> and in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_seas" title="High seas">high seas</a> outside these zones. They are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelagic" title="Pelagic">pelagic</a> species, which means they mostly live in the open ocean and do not live near the sea floor, although they may spend part of their life cycle in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nearshore_waters" title="Nearshore waters">nearshore waters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Capture_production">Capture production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelagic_fish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Capture production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">Food and Agriculture Organization</a> (FAO), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fishing_by_country" title="Fishing by country">world harvest</a> in 2005 consisted of 93.2 million <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a> captured by <a href="/wiki/Commercial_fishing" title="Commercial fishing">commercial fishing</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wild_fisheries" title="Wild fisheries">wild fisheries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Of this total, about 45% were pelagic fish. The following table shows the world capture production in <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonnes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Threatened_species">Threatened species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pelagic_fish&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Threatened species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 2009, the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> (IUCN) produced the first <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">red list</a> for threatened oceanic sharks and rays. They claim that about one third of open ocean sharks and rays are under <a href="/wiki/Threatened_species" title="Threatened species">threat of extinction</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> There are 64 species of oceanic sharks and rays on the list, including <a href="/wiki/Hammerhead_shark" title="Hammerhead shark">hammerheads</a>, giant <a href="/wiki/Mobula" title="Mobula">devil rays</a> and <a href="/wiki/Porbeagle" title="Porbeagle">porbeagle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>Oceanic sharks are <a href="/wiki/Incidental_catch" title="Incidental catch">captured incidentally</a> by swordfish and tuna <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_seas" title="High seas">high seas</a> fisheries. In the past there were few markets for sharks, which were regarded as worthless <a href="/wiki/Bycatch" title="Bycatch">bycatch</a>. Now sharks are being increasingly targeted to supply emerging Asian markets, particularly for <a href="/wiki/Shark_finning" title="Shark finning">shark fins</a>, which are used in <a href="/wiki/Shark_fin_soup" title="Shark fin soup">shark fin soup</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>The northwest Atlantic Ocean shark populations are estimated to have declined by 50% since the early 1970s. Oceanic sharks are vulnerable because they don't produce many young, and the young can take decades to mature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional center">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">

<p>In parts of the world the <a href="/wiki/Scalloped_hammerhead" title="Scalloped hammerhead">scalloped hammerhead</a> shark has declined by 99% since the late 1970s. Its status on the red list is that it is globally endangered, meaning it is near extinction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian_84-4"><a href="#cite_note-guardian-84">[84]</a></sup></p>

<ul>
<li>Collette, BB (2010) <a class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?hl=en&amp;lr=&amp;id=_4xZ_QoYNSwC&amp;oi=fnd&amp;pg=PA21" rel="nofollow">"Reproduction and development in epipelagic fishes"</a> In: Kathleen S Cole, <i>Reproduction and Sexuality in Marine Fishes: Patterns and Processes</i>, pp. 21–64, University of California Press. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-520-26433-5" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-520-26433-5">978-0-520-26433-5</a>.</li>

<li><cite class="citation journal">Makris, N; Ratilal, P; Jagannathan, S; Gong, Z; Andrews, M; Bertsatos, I; Godo, OR; Nero, RW; Jech, JM (2009). <a class="external text" href="http://www.sciencemag.org/cgi/content/abstract/323/5922/1734" rel="nofollow">"Critical Population Density Triggers Rapid Formation of Vast Oceanic Fish Shoals"</a>. <i>Science</i>. <b>323</b>: 1734–1737. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PubMed_Identifier" title="PubMed Identifier">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pubmed/19325116" rel="nofollow">19325116</a>. <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="//doi.org/10.1126%2Fscience.1169441" rel="nofollow">10.1126/science.1169441</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APelagic+fish&amp;rft.atitle=Critical+Population+Density+Triggers+Rapid+Formation+of+Vast+Oceanic+Fish+Shoals&amp;rft.au=Andrews%2C+M&amp;rft.au=Bertsatos%2C+I&amp;rft.au=Godo%2C+OR&amp;rft.au=Gong%2C+Z&amp;rft.au=Jagannathan%2C+S&amp;rft.au=Jech%2C+JM&amp;rft.au=Nero%2C+RW&amp;rft.au=Ratilal%2C+P&amp;rft.aufirst=N&amp;rft.aulast=Makris&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Science&amp;rft.pages=1734-1737&amp;rft.volume=323&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.sciencemag.org%2Fcgi%2Fcontent%2Fabstract%2F323%2F5922%2F1734&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1126%2Fscience.1169441&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F19325116&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal"><span style="display:none;"> </span></span></li>
